The Story of World Literature (1)(7)(2) by John Macy, translated by Hu Zhongchi 173

Chapter 44: Modern Spanish Literature

When we come to the most modern Spanish novels, this interest is undoubtedly the same as when talking about other modern literature. We cannot say much about modern Spaniards. In my opinion, existing readers must sift through the ashes themselves. This is not to say that anyone can take care of the stoves of the whole world, but that any intelligent reader can participate in the sifting.

Among the existing Spanish writers, the one who most faithfully adheres to his profession as a writer, his business, his art, is Pio Baroja. His "Market of the Meek" shows the conflicts between cities and many streets and alleys and people, as well as the appearance of indulgence and adventure. Moreover, Baroja has a sense of despairing opposition between different social classes. For example, in a novel (the English translation title "The Quest" is not at all the Spanish title, but the translation is excellent), he depicts the contrast between the unfortunate nightlife and daytime life of modern cities.

Generally speaking, young people oppose established social and political systems, and Spanish artists often mix their art with politics, which damages it. This is what the most excellent English critic of Spanish literature said. This may be true, but in other countries, the most outstanding and courageous artists have also opposed the government they dislike, or taken up swords or pens for the government they like. Think of Vigny, Hugo, the cavalier poets of 17th century England, Tolstoy, almost all Russians. The question is, what is the relationship between poetry and politics? Putting that aside, I think that one of the most interesting modern Spaniards, not very famous in English translation, is Unamuno, who is a essayist, satirist, magazine reporter, and poet. He has obtained the glory of being exiled from his home country. Exile is a form of punishment that modern governments rarely use when dealing with scholars. Why? Because exile immediately increases their reputation, ideas spread to the world, and those worthy of exile may be suspected of knowing how to retaliate with writing for this reason.

Another Spaniard who resisted the government is the famous writer Blasco Ibáñez. I am informed by astute critics that in Spain he is regarded as a buffoon. Indeed, he is an outstanding and high-class buffoon, creating his international reputation (or perhaps because several of his books have been translated into English and other national languages before, confirming that reputation). "The Four Horsemen of the Apocalypse" contains very moving dramatic scenes, cleverly embellished, appealing to the hearts of people who are in the midst of war or suffering because of war. However, since we are promised jokes, we might as well quote what the late W.D. Howells said about Blasco Ibáñez: "Ibáñez achieves effects beyond the art of Henry James; yet, even so, in lightness and beauty he falls below James."

Howells, who loved Spain and read many Spanish books, may have seen lightness and beauty in two contemporary Spaniards, Valle-Inclán and J.W. Ruiz, who calls himself Azorín. Valle-Inclán is subtle...

Chapter 45: Holland and Flanders, where Holland sleeps embraced by the sea.

Fly, oh my fantasy, to the hearts of those who feel differently.

I think of the place where the ocean rests against the land.

Her patient children are standing before my eyes.

—Goldsmith

Most of the writers mentioned in this short chapter are from before the 19th century, so we are trampling on the order of the ages. However, it seems most convenient to place this chapter, which is very strange and not overly important, here.

Why? Because Dutch literature is unimportant. Two erudite Dutchmen who generously taught me on this subject also take an almost disdainful attitude towards their own country's literature, especially later literature, and agree with this statement. Occasional visitors to European (and American) art galleries are surprised by the abundance of Dutch painting. Azorín, the author of "The Pleasant Recollections of the Duke of Braganza" and the beautiful trilogy based on the Carlist Wars, is an organic talent and valuable work. The author of "Confessions of a Little Philosopher" is destined to enter the world's library (if we can make fun of destiny). There is a lot of promise and achievement in modern Spanish literature. We can only hint at this before we must travel by plane to a country that political Spain did not treat with kindness in the past.

Literature.

Indeed, the Italians were the only competitors of the Dutch and Flemings during the Renaissance. The Italians were extremely great in all forms of literature, but the Dutch devoted all their efforts to arts other than literature. Or it may be that the Dutch language, like the neighboring German language, did not achieve a high degree of development until modern times and was always heavy and not flexible.

The two greatest Dutchmen, Spinoza and Erasmus, were humanists who did not use Dutch but wrote in Latin. Erasmus, a friend of Thomas More whom we met in an earlier chapter, like all humanists, had a tendency to turn back the clock and prevent the growth of modern languages. Why? Because in the eyes of humanists, all virtues lie in Greek and Latin. Erasmus was a scholar in the true sense of the word. He loved literature, especially classical literature, and especially ecclesiastical literature, because in ecclesiastical literature he saw what he, like most people at that time (remember that the year 1500 divided his seventy-year life into two equal parts), thought was the truth. The classicism of the Renaissance was mostly not Greek or Latin that ancient Romans could understand, but Christian. The best aspects of Erasmus are his letters and "Dialogues," a collection of dialogues on various subjects. It would not be clumsy criticism to put Plato's "Dialogues," Erasmus's "Dialogues," and Landor's "Imaginary Conversations" on the same bookshelf and look at them together, without saying a word about comparative value.

A century after Erasmus's death, Spinoza, who was neither Christian nor pagan but Jewish, was born in Amsterdam. Together with Bacon, Descartes, and Bruno, he was one of the founders of modern philosophy. It is questionable whether Spinoza belongs in our record, as I have indicated, because he was a rigorous, abstruse, and specialized philosopher who wrote in somewhat condensed scholarly Latin. His writing, according to Latin scholars, is that of a sound and precise thinker, but there is an artistic color there. (Erasmus had this, Bacon also had it, and in modern times Nietzsche, Bernard Shaw, William James, and Santayana also have it.) Scholars are there, but his method is lacking: a so-called philosophical geometry, setting out theorems with numbers, proofs, corollaries, and axioms. It was not until the beginning of the 19th century that philosophers and scholars such as Herder, Lessing, and Novalis rediscovered him. He was buried for a hundred years. Now he is one of the heroes of philosophical thought, and perhaps he does not completely lack literary color. The title of a part of his "Ethics," "On Human Bondage," was taken as the title of W. Somerset Maugham's most profound and beautiful modern novel.

In Dutch literature, as in all literature, we must go to legends, folk songs, and popular romances to seek the most fragrant materials. Holland at least contributed a world-famous translation of the "Reynard the Fox" poem by the minstrel Willem. This is one of the oldest translations of that famous story. It was translated into English from an undoubtedly Dutch prose version by Caxton. Such legends, romances, and satires move from one country to another. The question is only the skill of the adapter or importer, and now, in terms of novels, poetry, and criticism, that is still the only question. In medieval literature, the concept of ownership did not exist. The author did not need to apologize for adopting ideas seen anywhere. Reinaert (Reynard the Fox) seems to be the only great story that was popular in Dutch translation, and the most colorful modern version that was once very popular is Goethe's Reineckefuchs.

The greatest Dutch writer is the poet Vondel. He was born at the end of the 16th century and lived through three-quarters of the 17th century. He wrote tragedies that combined dramatic force and lyrical beauty. On the one hand, he is compared to Racine because he dramatized the heroic stories of the Bible. On the other hand, his masterpiece "Lucifer" links him with Milton. Milton seems to have at least the scholarly knowledge of that great Dutch drama, but it is doubtful what he took from it. "Paradise Lost," "Paradise Regained," and "Lucifer" have nothing in common except for the subject matter and the noble style. The two poets take completely different approaches. "Lucifer" is dramatic and lyrical, while Milton's poem is an epic narrative. A beautiful account of that poet and his political and religious background is in Professor A.J. Barnouw's biography of Vondel.

After Vondel's era, Dutch literature declined. Most of the works of the 18th century are very dull. A revival came at the end of that century and the beginning of the 19th century. The pioneer of the new era was the didactic poet Bilderdijk. His insightful poetry was very popular in Holland, but it did not make much of an impression in other countries. In England, he would be considered a second-rate poet. He was a serious scholar who earned the respect of such excellent critics as Robert Southey. However, from the fact that he thought Shakespeare lacked deliberation and disliked new German poetry, it can be inferred that his concept of aesthetic awareness was limited.

Market News 174

The Chilean National Fertilizer Company announced that on the 25th of this month, the Chilean National Congress resolved to organize the company to promote the business of the country's fertilizer plants. The company has a capital of 75 million pounds sterling, with a term of 60 years. Half of the capital belongs to the government, and the fertilizer-producing land is used as a reciprocal condition. The other half is used to exchange stocks of existing fertilizer companies. The company's primary purpose is to promote the construction of two large new fertilizer plants, each capable of producing 700,000 tons of fertilizer per year. The new plants will adopt the most economical method, which is the one adopted by one of the existing plants.

Yianju on Nanjing Road and Shilu Crossing announced that this year, the company has shipped dozens of varieties of Xiangyunsha from Guangdong, all of which are the company's orders. The texture is fine and clean, and it will never fade. It is very different from the ordinary goods on the market. Now it is sold at a fair price. In addition, Bingqilian has been tested as the first by the Public Concession Health Department over the years, so this year it is especially clean, fresh, and moist. The decoration of the two-story ice room is also unique, so that customers feel cool and refreshed as soon as they enter, without having to drink ice.

This film is known as the history of the Sai Xuan Palace, and it is extraordinary. After watching it, those who keep the ticket will have the hope of winning a prize. For details, please read the advertisement of this theater.
